Let N have at least three proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then d1 = p. Let d2 be the second smallest divisor >1. There are two cases: either N is a prime power p^k with k>=3, or N has at least two distinct prime factors.

Case 1: N = p^k, k>=3. Proper divisors: 1, p, p^2, ..., p^{k-1}. The three largest proper divisors are p^{k-1}, p^{k-2}, p^{k-3}. Their sum S(N) = p^{k-1}+p^{k-2}+p^{k-3} = p^{k-3}(p^2+p+1). Compare to N = p^k. So S(N) = N * (p^2+p+1)/p^3 = N * (1/p + 1/p^2 + 1/p^3). For p=2, S(2^k)=2^{k-1}+2^{k-2}+2^{k-3}=2^{k-3}(4+2+1)=7*2^{k-3}. Compare to N=2^k. So S(N)=N * 7/8? Actually 2^{k-3}*7 = (2^k)*7/8 = 0.875N. So S(N) < N for p=2. For p=3, S(3^k)=3^{k-1}+3^{k-2}+3^{k-3}=3^{k-3}(9+3+1)=13*3^{k-3}= (3^k)*13/27 ≈ 0.481N. So S(N) < N. For p>=5, S(N) = p^{k-3}(p^2+p+1) < p^k = N, because p^2+p+1 < p^3 for p>=2? Actually p^3 - (p^2+p+1) = p^3 - p^2 - p -1 = p^2(p-1) - (p+1). For p=5: 125-25-5-1=94>0, so yes. So for prime powers, S(N) < N. So the sequence decreases strictly until it reaches a number that is not a prime power? Actually if N is a prime power with k>=3, then S(N) is also a prime power? Case 2: N has at least two distinct prime factors. Let p be the smallest prime factor. Then d1 = p. The second smallest divisor >1 could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or q, the next smallest prime factor (which is > p). The third smallest divisor >1 could be either p^3 if p^3 divides N, or p*q, or q^2, or p^2, etc., depending on the factorization. In general, the three smallest divisors >1 are the three smallest numbers in the set of divisors of N that are >1. They are the three smallest numbers in the divisor set (excluding 1). So we can denote them as d1 < d2 < d3.

Then S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now note that N/d1, N/d2, N/d3 are the three largest proper divisors. Since d1 is the smallest prime factor, N/d1 is the largest proper divisor. Also, N/d2 and N/d3 are the second and third largest proper divisors. So S(N) is the sum of the three largest proper divisors.

Let's attempt to analyze the function S(N) more systematically. Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Let p be the smallest prime factor of N. Then the divisors of N include 1, p, and then possibly p^2, p^3, ... and other primes. The three smallest divisors >1 are: d1 = p. d2 = min(p^2 if p^2|N, q the next prime factor). d3 = min(p^3 if p^3|N, p*q if both p and q divide N, q^2 if q^2|N, etc.). So S(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.

Now note that N/d1 = N/p, which is the largest proper divisor. N/d2 and N/d3 are the second and third largest proper divisors.

We can consider different types of numbers based on their prime factorization. The key observation is that if N is a fixed point, then S(N)=N, which implies 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3 = 1. Thus, the only numbers that produce infinite sequences are: fixed points (numbers of the form 2*3^k and 2*3*p with p prime > 6) and numbers that map to these fixed points in one step, i.e., numbers of the form 2^3*3^k for k>=2. But also, could there be numbers that map to a fixed point after two steps, like N such that S(N)=M where M is a preimage of a fixed point? For instance, if there is an N such that S(N)=72, then N would map to 72 then to 78 fixed. But we found no N maps to 72. So the preimage tree stops at that level. Similarly, it seems that the only numbers that produce infinite sequences are fixed points and numbers that map to fixed points in one step. But we must also consider numbers that map to numbers that map to fixed points? That would be two-step preimages. We saw no preimages for 72. Could there be a number that maps to 216? That would be a two-step preimage of 78? Actually 216->234 fixed, so N such that S(N)=216 would give N->216->234 fixed. But we found no N with S(N)=216. So the tree is just one level.

Thus, the possible a1 are: all numbers N such that either S(N)=N (fixed points) or S(N) is a fixed point. But wait, could there be numbers that are not in the tree but still produce infinite sequence because they lead to a cycle of length >1 that does not hit a prime? That would require a cycle where each term has at least three proper divisors. From our computational search, we haven't found any cycles other than fixed points. Could there be a cycle of length 2? Let's try to see if there exist N and M distinct such that S(N)=M and S(M)=N. Suppose N is even, p=2. Then S(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. For M to be distinct, we need S(N) != N. Also S(M)=N. This is a system. We can try to see if any small numbers satisfy. From our earlier calculations, we have S(12)=13, S(13) undefined; S(24)=26, S(26)=16; S(30)=31; S(36)=39, S(39)=17; S(40)=38, S(38)=22; S(42)=42 fixed; S(48)=52, S(52)=43; S(54)=54 fixed; S(56)=50, S(50)=40; S(60)=65, S(65)=19; S(66)=66 fixed; S(68)=55, S(55)=17; S(70)=59; S(72)=78, S(78)=78 fixed; S(74)=40; S(75)=45; S(76)=61; S(78)=78 fixed; S(80)=76; S(84)=91, S(91)=21; S(86)=46; S(87)=33; S(88)=77; S(90)=93, S(93)=35; S(92)=73; S(93)=35; S(94)=50; S(95)=25; S(96)=104, S(104)=91; S(98)=70; S(99)=53; S(100)=95; So no 2-cycle.

What about a cycle of length 3? Possibly? Unlikely.

Thus, it seems the only way to have infinite sequence is to eventually become a fixed point. Since the fixed points are infinite, the set of a1 is infinite. The problem likely expects a description of all possible a1. Perhaps the answer is: a1 can be any positive integer that is not a prime or a prime square, and also not of the form 2^a * 3^b * something? But we have a lot of numbers that fail, like 12,20,24,30, etc. So we need a precise classification.

Maybe we can prove that the only numbers that can appear in an infinite sequence are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5? But 12 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, yet fails because it's divisible by 4. So the condition is: a1 is divisible by 6, and either not divisible by 4, or if divisible by 4, then it must be of the form 2^3*3^k with k>=2? But that is too specific. So the only numbers that work are those that eventually land on a fixed point, and the fixed points themselves are those with v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1. And the preimage tree of these fixed points consists of numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b * u, where a and b satisfy certain relations. It seems that the only numbers that can appear in the infinite sequence are those that are of the form 2^a * 3^b * u, with u odd and not divisible by 5, and also that the exponent a is such that when you apply S repeatedly, you eventually get to a number with v2=1 and v3>=1. This is reminiscent of a classification: the only numbers with infinite sequences are those that are not divisible by 5 and that after some steps, the exponent of 2 becomes 1 and the exponent of 3 becomes at least 1. But we have seen that numbers like 12 are divisible by 4 and 3, and S(12)=13, which is prime, so fails. So 12 does not work. So the condition is more subtle.

Maybe we can try to characterize the set of numbers that never reach a prime or prime square. Let's denote the function S(N). For N even, we have three subcases:

- If N is a multiple of 12 (i.e., v2>=2 and v3>=1), then S(N) = 13N/12. So S(N) is also a multiple of 13. Then we can iterate: S^2(N) = 13/12 * S(N) = 13/12 * 13N/12 = (13^2/12^2)N. In general, if N is divisible by 12, then the sequence under S will be N, 13N/12, (13/12)^2 N, ... as long as the numbers remain divisible by 12? But careful: If N is divisible by 12, then 13N/12 is integer, but is it divisible by 12? Not necessarily. For N=12, 13N/12=13, not divisible by 12. So the iteration may break the divisibility by 12. So the dynamics depend on the exponent of 2 and 3.

- If N is a multiple of 4 but not of 3 (v2>=2, v3=0), then S(N) = (3N/4) + N/d3, where d3 is the smallest odd divisor of n (the odd part). This is more complicated.

- If N is even but not a multiple of 4 (v2=1), then S(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3, where d2 and d3 are divisors of n (the odd part). This is also complex.

Now, what about numbers like 72? It works. So the set of good numbers includes fixed points and numbers of the form 8*3^k*u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u. But also, could there be numbers that map to these preimages? That is, numbers N such that S(N)=M, where M is a preimage of a fixed point? Then N would also work. For example, is there N such that S(N)=72? We already looked for S(N)=72 and found none. But maybe there is N such that S(N)=216? Let's try to solve S(N)=216. We already attempted and found no integer solutions. But we need to be systematic. Let's consider the equation S(N)=t, where t is of the form 8*3^k*u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u. We can try to see if such N exist. For instance, can we have N such that S(N)=216? If N is a multiple of 12, then S(N)=13N/12, so N=12*216/13=2592/13 not integer. If N has v2>=2 and 3∤N, then S(N)= (3N/4)+N/d3. For S(N)=216, we would need N = 4*216/(3+4/d3) = 864/(3+4/d3). This is messy. We can attempt to solve the equation S(N)=216 by considering possible triples. For N even, d1=2. Then d2 and d3 are divisors. The sum S(N)=N/2+N/d2+N/d3. So N/2+N/d2+N/d3=216 => N(1/2+1/d2+1/d3)=216. So N = 216/(1/2+1/d2+1/d3) = 432/(1+2/d2+2/d3). So N must be integer. For each candidate (d2,d3) with d2<d3, both >1, and such that N is even and has the correct divisor structure, we can compute N. This is similar to the inverse problem. We can try to find all solutions. Let's denote x=2/d2, y=2/d3. Then N=432/(1+x+y). Since d2,d3 are integers, x and y are rational numbers of the form 2/n. The possible values for x and y are: 2/3, 2/4=1/2, 2/5=0.4, 2/6=1/3, 2/7≈0.2857, etc. The sum 1+x+y must be a divisor of 432. We can try to find all pairs (d2,d3) such that 1+2/d2+2/d3 divides 432? Actually N = 432/(1+2/d2+2/d3). For N to be integer, 1+2/d2+2/d3 must be a rational number that simplifies to a fraction with numerator dividing 432. Better: Write N = 432 * d2 d3 / (d2 d3 + 2d3 + 2d2). So N = 432 * d2 d3 / (d2 d3 + 2d2 + 2d3). For N to be integer, the denominator must divide the numerator. Also, N must be even and have the three smallest divisors 2, d2, d3. This is a Diophantine equation. We can try to find solutions for d2,d3. For t=216, we can attempt to find integer N such that S(N)=216. From our earlier computed examples, we have S(72)=26, S(216)=234, S(504)=? 504 gives S=546, not 216. So 216 does not appear as S(N) for any N we tested. But maybe there is a solution. Let's attempt to solve the equation S(N)=t for general t. The problem likely expects a characterization of all a1 for which the sequence is infinite. Given the complexity, maybe there is a simpler observation: The sequence will eventually become periodic or hit a prime. The only way to have an infinite sequence is to eventually become a fixed point. And the fixed points are exactly numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. Moreover, the preimage of these fixed points are numbers of the form 12*(F/13) where F is a fixed point divisible by 13. But those preimages themselves are not fixed points. However, could there be numbers that map to these preimages? That would require solving S(N)=M for M in that set. From the structure, it seems that the only numbers that can appear in the infinite sequence are those that are in the "good" set G, which is the set of numbers that never reach a prime or prime square under iteration of S. This set G is exactly the set of numbers that are either fixed points or map to fixed points in one step. But we need to verify if any number maps to a preimage (i.e., S(N)=72) because if such N exists, then N would also be in G. We have not found any N such that S(N)=72. Let's try to solve S(N)=72. Write N=2^a*n with n odd. The equation N/2+N/d2+N/d3=72. Multiply by 2: N+2N/d2+2N/d3=144. So N(1+2/d2+2/d3)=144. So N = 144/(1+2/d2+2/d3). For N to be integer, 1+2/d2+2/d3 must be a rational number that divides 144. Let's try possible d2,d3. Since d2 and d3 are divisors of N>1, they are at least 3. Let's list possible (d2,d3) and compute N. For d2=3,d3=6: 1+2/3+2/6=1+0.666+0.333=2, N=72. So N=72 is a solution with d2=3,d3=6. But then we need that N's three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. For N=72, that is true because 72 is divisible by 2,3,6? Actually 72/4=18, so 4 is a divisor, and 4<6, so the three smallest are 2,3,4, not 2,3,6. So the triple (2,3,6) is not the three smallest divisors because 4 is a divisor. So the equation S(N)=72 with d2=3,d3=6 would require that the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. That would be the case if N is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4, and also not by 5? Actually if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then it's divisible by 6. But if it's also divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor and 4<6, so the third smallest would be 4, not 6. So to have the triple (2,3,6), we need that N is not divisible by 4. Also, we need that the next smallest divisor after 3 is 6, i.e., no divisor between 3 and 6, which is true if N is not divisible by 4 or 5. So N must have v2=1, v3>=1, and v5=0. That is exactly a fixed point. So the solution (d2=3,d3=6) corresponds to N being a fixed point. So N=72 is not a fixed point, so the triple (2,3,6) does not apply to N=72 because 4 divides 72. So the equation S(N)=72 with d2=3,d3=6 does not give a valid N because the assumption that d2 and d3 are the three smallest divisors fails. So we need to consider only triples that are actually the three smallest divisors of N. For N=72, the three smallest are 2,3,4, so d2=3,d3=4. So the equation S(N)=72 would be N/2+N/3+N/4=72 => N(1/2+1/3+1/4)=72 => N*13/12=72 => N=72*12/13=864/13 not integer. So no.

Thus, to find N such that S(N)=t, we need to consider all possible triples (d1,d2,d3) that are the three smallest divisors of N, and then solve N(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=t. This is a specific Diophantine equation. For t=72, the possible triples (2,3,4) gives N=72*12/13 not integer; (2,3,5) gives N=72*? Actually 1/2+1/3+1/5=31/30, N=72*30/31=2160/31 not integer; (2,3,6) gives N=72*? 1/2+1/3+1/6=1, N=72, but then we need that N's three smallest are 2,3,6, which requires that N is not divisible by 4 or 5. For N=72, it's divisible by 4, so not. (2,4,5) gives 1/2+1/4+1/5=19/20, N=72*20/19=1440/19 not integer; (2,4,7) gives 1/2+1/4+1/7=25/28, N=72*28/25=2016/25 not integer; etc. So no integer N.

Thus, it seems that the only numbers that can appear in infinite sequences are those that are either fixed points or numbers that map to fixed points in one step (i.e., S(N) is a fixed point). And those numbers that map to fixed points are exactly those of the form 2^3 * 3^k * u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u. But also, could there be numbers that map to these numbers? That would be a chain of length 2. We need to see if any N maps to a number of the form 2^3 * 3^k * u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u. For example, can we have N such that S(N)=72? We already tried and found no. For N such that S(N)=216? Let's try to solve S(N)=216. Possible triples (2,3,4) gives N=216*12/13=2592/13 not integer. (2,3,5) gives N=216*30/31=6480/31 not integer. (2,3,6) gives N=216, but then need N's three smallest 2,3,6, which would require N not divisible by 4 or 5. For N=216, it's divisible by 4? 216/4=54, so v2=3, so 4 divides, so triple is (2,3,4) not (2,3,6). So not. (2,4,5) gives N=216*20/19=4320/19 not integer. (2,4,7) gives N=216*28/25=6048/25 not integer. (2,4,8) gives 1/2+1/4+1/8=7/8, N=216*8/7=1728/7 not integer. (2,4,9) gives 1/2+1/4+1/9=25/36, N=216*36/25=7776/25 not integer. So no.

What about S(N)=504? 504=2^3*3^2*7, so triple (2,3,4) gives N=504*12/13=6048/13 not integer; (2,3,5) gives 504*30/31=15120/31 not integer; (2,3,6) gives 504, but then need N not divisible by 4, but 504 divisible by 4? 504/4=126, so v2=3, so triple is (2,3,4) not (2,3,6). So no.

Thus, it appears that the only numbers that are in the preimage tree of fixed points are those that are themselves of the form 2^3 * 3^k * u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u, and that these have no further preimages. So the set G is exactly the union of fixed points and these numbers. In other words, the possible a1 are all numbers N such that either

- N is a multiple of 6, N is not a multiple of 4, and N is not a multiple of 5 (i.e., N = 6*m with m odd and 5∤m), or
- N is a multiple of 8, N is a multiple of 9? Actually N = 2^3 * 3^k * u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u. (Note that if k=2, then N=8*9*u=72u, so N is a multiple of 72. If u=1, N=72,216,648,... but also for u=7, N=504, etc.)

But is there any overlap? No, because fixed points have v2=1, while these have v2=3. So they are disjoint.

Now, we should also consider numbers that are not even? Could an odd number produce an infinite sequence? Let's test an odd number like 3,5,7,9,11,... The smallest odd composite is 9, but 9 has only two proper divisors (1,3), so not allowed. 15 has three proper divisors (1,3,5), but S(15)=1+3+5=9, which is prime square, then fails. 21: S=11+3+1=15, then 15->9, then 9 fails. 25: fails. 27: S=9+3+1=13 prime, fails. 33: S=11+3+1=15, then 15->9 fails. 35: S=7+5+1=13 prime fails. 39: S=13+3+1=17 prime fails. 45: S=15+9+5=29 prime fails. 49: fails. 51: S=17+3+1=21, then 21->15->9 fails. 55: S=11+5+1=17 fails. 57: S=19+3+1=23 fails. 63: S=21+9+7=37 fails. 65: S=13+5+1=19 fails. 69: S=23+3+1=27, then 27->13 fail. 75: S=25+15+5=45, then 45->29 fail. 77: S=11+7+1=19 fail. 81: S=27+9+3=39, then 39->17 fail. 85: S=17+5+1=23 fail. 87: S=29+3+1=33, then 33->15->9 fail. 91: S=13+7+1=21, then 21->15->9 fail. 93: S=31+3+1=35, then 35->13 fail. 95: S=19+5+1=25 fail. 99: S=33+9+3=45, then 45->29 fail. So no odd number seems to work. Could there be an odd number that is a fixed point? For odd N, the smallest prime factor is at least 3. Then S(N)=N/p+N/q+N/r, which is less than N/3+N/3+N/3=N, but also N is odd, so S(N) is less than N. If N is a fixed point, then S(N)=N, which would require 1/p+1/q+1/r=1. Since p,q,r are at least 3, the maximum sum of reciprocals is 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, which would require p=q=r=3. So if N is a fixed point, we need that the three smallest divisors >1 are 3,3,3? But divisors are distinct, so cannot have three equal divisors. So no odd fixed point. Thus, only even numbers can work. And we have characterized even numbers that work.

But we need to be rigorous: prove that the only numbers that lead to an infinite sequence are those that are either fixed points (S(N)=N) or numbers that map to a fixed point in one step (S(N) is a fixed point). And then characterize these numbers.

From our computational evidence, it seems that numbers that map to fixed points are exactly those of the form 8*3^k*u with k>=2 and u odd, 5∤u. And fixed points are numbers of the form 6*m with m odd and 5∤m. But we should also consider numbers that map to a fixed point after more steps? That would be numbers N such that S(N)=M, where M is a preimage of a fixed point (i.e., S(M) is a fixed point). If such N exists, then N would also be in G. But we haven't found any such N. Could there be a solution? Let's try to see if we can find N such that S(N)=72. We already tried and found none. For S(N)=216, we found none. For S(N)=504, none. So it seems that the preimage set of numbers of the form 8*3^k*u (k>=2) is empty. This is plausible because the function S(N) for N even has a specific form: if N is a multiple of 12, then S(N)=13N/12, which is not of the form 8*3^k*u because 13N/12 would have v2? For N=12, S=13; for N=24, S=26; for N=36, S=39; for N=48, S=52; for N=60, S=65; for N=72, S=78; for N=84, S=91; for N=96, S=104; for N=108, S=117; for N=120, S=130; for N=132, S=143; for N=144, S=156; for N=156, S=169; for N=168, S=182; for N=180, S=195; for N=192, S=208; for N=204, S=221; for N=216, S=234; for N=228, S=247; for N=240, S=260; for N=252, S=273; for N=264, S=286; for N=276, S=299; for N=288, S=312; etc. So S(N) for N divisible by 12 is of the form 13N/12, which is often not divisible by 8 or 9. For N=72, S=78; 78 is not divisible by 8. So the numbers that are multiples of 12 are not in the set {8*3^k*u}. But could a number not divisible by 12 map to 72? For instance, if N has v2=1 and 3|N, then S(N)=N, so N would be a fixed point, but 72 is not a fixed point. If N has v2=1 and 3∤N, then S(N)=N/2+N/p+N/2p? That's complex. Let's attempt to solve S(N)=72 directly by enumerating possible N. Since N is even, we can try N from 2 to 144. But we already did a systematic search earlier for N up to 144 and found that only 72 itself? Actually we computed S(N) for N up to 144 and saw that S(12)=13, S(24)=26, S(36)=39, S(48)=52, S(60)=65, S(72)=78, S(84)=91, S(96)=104, S(108)=117, S(120)=130, S(132)=143, S(144)=156. None give 72. For N=8, S=7; N=10, S=8; N=14, S=10; N=16, S=14; N=20, S=19; N=22, S=14; N=26, S=16; N=28, S=25; N=30, S=31; N=32, S=28; N=34, S=20; N=38, S=22; N=40, S=38; N=44, S=37; N=46, S=26; N=50, S=40; N=52, S=43; N=56, S=50; N=58, S=32; N=62, S=34; N=64, S=56; N=66, S=66; N=68, S=55; N=70, S=59; N=74, S=40; N=76, S=61; N=78, S=78; N=80, S=76; N=82, S=44; N=84, S=91; N=86, S=46; N=88, S=77; N=90, S=93; N=92, S=73; N=94, S=50; N=98, S=70; N=100, S=95; N=102, S=102; N=104, S=91; N=106, S=56; N=108, S=117; N=110, S=87; N=112, S=100; N=114, S=114; N=116, S=91; N=118, S=62; N=120, S=130; N=122, S=64; N=124, S=97; N=126, S=126; N=128, S=112; N=130, S=88; N=132, S=143; N=134, S=70; N=136, S=119; N=138, S=138; N=140, S=133; N=142, S=74; N=144, S=156. So no S=72. So indeed, no N maps to 72. Similarly, for other numbers, we can check if any N maps to 216. We already did some enumeration; likely none. So the preimage tree stops at depth 1.

Thus, the set of possible a1 is exactly the set of numbers N such that either S(N)=N or S(N) is a fixed point. And these numbers are:

- Fixed points: all numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, but not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5. (This is equivalent to N = 6*m where m is odd and 5∤m.)
- Numbers that map to fixed points: all numbers N such that N is divisible by 8, N is divisible by 9? Actually S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12, which is a fixed point if 13N/12 is a fixed point. For this to happen, we need that 13N/12 is a fixed point, i.e., v2(13N/12)=1, v5=0, v3>=1. Since 13 is odd, v2(13N/12)=v2(N)-2 (because 12=4*3, v2(12)=2). So we need v2(N)-2 = 1 => v2(N)=3. Also, v5(13N/12)=v5(N) (since 13 and 12 not divisible by 5) must be 0, so 5∤N. Also, v3(13N/12)=v3(N)-1 (since 12 has one factor 3) must be at least 1, so v3(N)>=2. So N must satisfy v2(N)=3, v3(N)>=2, and v5(N)=0. So N is of the form 2^3 * 3^k * u, where k>=2 and u is odd and not divisible by 5. Also, note that N must have at least three proper divisors, which is true for N>6. So this is the condition.

Now, are there any other ways for N to map to a fixed point? For example, could N have v2=1 and 3|N, then S(N)=N, so N would be a fixed point itself, not mapping to another fixed point. Could N have v2=1 and 3∤N, then S(N)=N/2+N/p+N/2p? That's less than N, and might equal a fixed point. For N=2*5=10, S=8; 8 is not fixed. N=2*7=14, S=10; 10 not fixed. N=2*11=22, S=14; 14 not fixed. N=2*13=26, S=16; 16 not fixed. N=2*17=34, S=20; 20 not fixed. N=2*19=38, S=22; 22 not fixed. N=2*23=46, S=26; 26 not fixed. N=2*29=58, S=32; 32 not fixed. N=2*31=62, S=34; 34 not fixed. N=2*37=74, S=40; 40 not fixed. N=2*41=82, S=44; 44 not fixed. N=2*43=86, S=46; 46 not fixed. N=2*47=94, S=50; 50 not fixed. N=2*49=98, S=56; 56 not fixed. So none give fixed points. So the only way to map to a fixed point is when N is a multiple of 12 and 13N/12 is a fixed point. But what about N that are multiples of 4 but not 3? Then S(N)=N/2+N/4+N/d3, with d3 the smallest odd divisor of n. This could potentially equal a fixed point. For example, take N=20, v2=2, v3=0, d3=5 (since 20=4*5, n=5, smallest odd prime factor is 5). Then S=10+5+4=19, not fixed. N=40, v2=3? Actually 40=8*5, v2=3, so it's a multiple of 8, not 4? Actually v2=3, so N is divisible by 8, so it's in the previous case? For N=40, v2=3, v3=0, but then the three smallest divisors: 2,4,5? Since v2=3, 4 divides N, and 8? Actually 40/2=20, /4=10, /5=8? Wait 40 divisors: 1,2,4,5,8,10,20,40. Three smallest >1: 2,4,5. So d2=4, d3=5. Then S=20+10+8=38, not fixed. So N=40 does not map to a fixed point. What about N=56, v2=3, v3=0, d2=4, d3=7? S=28+14+8=50, not fixed. So no. What about N=60, v2=2, v3=1, so it's a multiple of 12, S=65, not fixed. So the only numbers that map to fixed points are those with v2=3, v3>=2. That is, N=8*3^k*u with k>=2, u odd, 5∤u.

Thus, the complete characterization: The possible a1 are all positive integers N such that either

- N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5 (i.e., N = 6*m with m odd and 5∤m), or
- N is divisible by 8, N is divisible by 9? Actually N is of the form 8*3^k*u, where k>=2 and u is odd, and 5∤u.

But note: In the second condition, if k=2, then N=72*u, and u can be any odd number not divisible by 5. Also, we should consider if N is a fixed point but also satisfies the second condition? No, because fixed points have v2=1, while second condition has v2=3. So they are disjoint.

Now, we need to check if there are any numbers that map to a fixed point in more than one step? For instance, could there be N such that S(N)=M, where M is a number of the form 8*3^k*u (k>=2, u odd, 5∤u)? Then N would also work. But we have not found any such N. Let's try to see if there is any N such that S(N)=72. We already searched N up to 144 and found none. Could there be N > 144 with S(N)=72? Since S(N) is usually less than N, but could be greater. For N=24, S=26; N=30, S=31; N=36, S=39; N=48, S=52; N=60, S=65; N=72, S=78; N=84, S=91; N=96, S=104; N=108, S=117; N=120, S=130; N=132, S=143; N=144, S=156; So S(N) for N multiple of 12 is increasing. For N=24, S=26; N=36, S=39; N=48, S=52; N=60, S=65; N=72, S=78; N=84, S=91; N=96, S=104; N=108, S=117; N=120, S=130; N=132, S=143; N=144, S=156; So S(N) is always greater than 72 for N>=72? Actually 72 maps to 78>72, so S(N) is >72 for N>=72? For N=72, S=78>72. For N=84, S=91>72. So for N divisible by 12 and N>=72, S(N) >72. For N=60, S=65<72. So there is a gap: N=60 gives S=65, N=72 gives S=78. So S(N) jumps from 65 to 78, skipping 72. So no N divisible by 12 gives S=72. For N with v2=1 and 3|N, S(N)=N, so N would need to be 72, but 72 is not such N. For N with v2=2 and 3|N, then N is multiple of 12, already considered. For N with v2=2 and 3∤N, then N=4n with n odd and 3∤n. Then S(N)=N/2+N/4+N/d3 = 2n+n+4n/d3 = 3n+4n/d3 = n(3+4/d3). For S(N)=72, we need n(3+4/d3)=72. Since d3 is odd and at least 5 (because 3∤n), possible d3=5,7,11,... Then 3+4/d3 = 3+0.8=3.8, 3+0.5714=3.5714, etc. Then n = 72/(3+4/d3) = 72/( (3d3+4)/d3 ) = 72d3/(3d3+4). For d3=5: n=360/19≈18.947, not integer. d3=7: n=504/25=20.16, no. d3=11: n=792/37≈21.405, no. d3=13: n=936/43≈21.767, no. d3=17: n=1224/55≈22.254, no. d3=19: n=1368/61≈22.459, no. d3=23: n=1656/73≈22.71, no. d3=29: n=2088/91≈22.967, no. So no.

For N with v2=1 and 3∤N, then N=2n with n odd and 3∤n. Then S(N)=n + N/p + N/2p? Actually the three smallest divisors: 2, p, 2p where p is the smallest odd prime factor of n. Then S(N)=N/2+N/p+N/(2p)=n + 2n/p + n/p = n + 3n/p = n(1+3/p). For S(N)=72, we need n(1+3/p)=72. p is odd prime >=5. Then n = 72/(1+3/p) = 72p/(p+3). For p=5: n=360/8=45, N=90. Check N=90: 90=2*45, divisors: 2,3,5? Actually 90/2=45, /3=30, /5=18, so the three smallest are 2,3,5? Wait 90 is divisible by 3, so 3 is a divisor, and 3<5, so the triple is (2,3,5) not (2,5,10). So our assumption that p is the smallest odd prime factor fails because 3 divides n, so p=3, not 5. So N=90 is not in this case. For p=7: n=504/10=50.4, no. p=11: n=792/14=56.571, no. So no.

Thus, indeed, no N maps to 72. Similarly, for other numbers, we can argue that the equation S(N)=t has no solutions for t in the set {8*3^k*u} with k>=2. This is plausible because the function S(N) for N even has a specific structure: if N is a multiple of 12, then S(N)=13N/12, which is not of that form because 13N/12 would have v2? For N=12, S=13; for N=24, S=26; etc. So these are not multiples of 8. If N is a multiple of 4 but not 3, then S(N) = 3n+4n/d3. For this to be a multiple of 8, we would need some conditions. It might be possible for some n. But we need to check if any such S(N) could be exactly a number of the form 8*3^k*u. For example, could S(N)=72? No. For S(N)=216? Possibly? Let's try to see if there is any N such that S(N)=216. We can attempt to solve the equation N(1/d1+1/d2+1/d3)=216. For N even, d1=2. We need to find all possible (d2,d3) such that N is integer and the triple is the three smallest divisors. This is a finite search. We can try to solve for d2,d3 with d2<d3, both >1, and such that N = 216/(1/2+1/d2+1/d3) = 432/(1+2/d2+2/d3). For N to be even, we need that the fraction simplifies. We can try all d2 from 3 to, say, 20. But we already did some. Let's systematically attempt to find all integer N such that S(N)=216. We can also use the fact that if N is divisible by 12, then S=13N/12, so N=216*12/13=2592/13 not integer. If N is divisible by 4 but not 3, then d2=4, d3 is the smallest odd divisor of n (n=N/4). Then S=3n+4n/d3 = n(3+4/d3). For this to equal 216, n = 216/(3+4/d3) = 216d3/(3d3+4). For d3=5: n=1080/19≈56.84; d3=7: n=1512/25=60.48; d3=11: n=2376/37≈64.16; d3=13: n=2808/43≈65.35; d3=17: n=3672/55≈66.76; d3=19: n=4104/61≈67.34; d3=23: n=4968/73≈68.08; d3=29: n=6264/91≈68.82; d3=31: n=6696/97≈69.14; d3=37: n=7992/115≈69.55; none integer. For d3=3? But 3|n? No, we are in case 3∤n, so d3 cannot be 3. So no.

If N is divisible by 2 but not 4, then d2 is the smallest odd prime factor p of n (n=N/2), and d3=2p (since 2p divides N). Then S = n + n/p + n/(2p) = n(1+1/p+1/(2p)) = n( (2p+2+p)/(2p) ) = n(3p+2)/(2p). So N = 2n, S = N/2 * (3p+2)/(2p) = N(3p+2)/(4p). For S=216, N = 216*4p/(3p+2) = 864p/(3p+2). For p=5: N=4320/17≈254.12; p=7: N=6048/23≈263.3; p=11: N=9504/35≈271.7; p=13: N=11232/41≈273.8; p=17: N=14688/53≈277.1; p=19: N=16416/59≈278.1; p=23: N=19872/71≈280.1; none integer. So no.

Thus, no N maps to 216. Similarly, for other numbers of the form 8*3^k*u, we can argue that the equation S(N)=t has no solutions because the denominator (1+2/d2+2/d3) would need to be a rational number that divides 2t, and the only possible triples that give t as S(N) are those where t is itself a fixed point or a preimage of a fixed point, but we have already exhausted. This is getting heavy.

Given the problem is likely from an IMO Shortlist or similar, there is probably a simpler approach: Show that the only numbers that can appear in the infinite sequence are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, or numbers that are multiples of 72? But we have 24? No, 24 fails. So only numbers that are multiples of 72? 72, 216, 648, etc. But also 504 works, which is 8*3^2*7. So it's not just multiples of 72; it's numbers with v2=3 and v3>=2. So the set G is: { N: v2(N)=1, v5(N)=0, v3(N)>=1 } ∪ { N: v2(N)=3, v5(N)=0, v3(N)>=2 }.

But we should also consider numbers with v2=3 and v3=1? That would be N=8*3=24, which fails. So the second set requires v3>=2. So the classification is: a1 must satisfy either

- v2(a1)=1, v5(a1)=0, v3(a1)>=1, or
- v2(a1)=3, v5(a1)=0, v3(a1)>=2.

Now, is there any other possibility? Could a number with v2=2 and v3=2 work? That would be N=4*9=36, which fails. So no.

Thus, the answer is: all possible a1 are positive integers N such that either (i) N is divisible by 6, but not by 4, and not by 5; or (ii) N is divisible by 8, and by 9? Actually N is divisible by 8 and by 9? Because v2=3 means divisible by 8, and v3>=2 means divisible by 9. So N is divisible by 72, but not necessarily by 72? 72=8*9, so N must be divisible by 72. And also not divisible by 5. So condition (ii) is: N is divisible by 72, and not divisible by 5. But also, if N is divisible by 72, then v2(N)>=3 and v3(N)>=2. However, it's possible that v2(N)>3, e.g., N=144? But 144 has v2=4, v3=2. Does it satisfy condition (ii)? v2=4, not 3. So N=144 is not in the set. So we need v2(N)=3 exactly? Let's check: N=72*u with u odd and 5∤u. For u=1, N=72, v2=3, v3=2. For u=7, N=504, v2=3, v3=2? 504=8*63=2^3*3^2*7, so v2=3, v3=2. For u=3, N=216, v2=3, v3=3. So v2 is exactly 3. Could there be a number with v2=3 and v3>=2 but also other primes? Yes, e.g., N=8*9*7=504, works. So condition (ii) is: N is divisible by 72, and the exponent of 2 in N is exactly 3 (i.e., N is exactly divisible by 8 but not by 16), and N is not divisible by 5. But also, if N is divisible by 72, then it is divisible by 8, so v2>=3. For v2 to be exactly 3, we need that the odd part is not divisible by 2, so that's fine. But could N have v2=3 and still be a multiple of 72? 72=2^3*3^2, so any multiple of 72 has v2>=3. If the multiple is, say, 144, then v2=4, so it's not in the set. So the condition is that N is a multiple of 72, but the factor 2^3 is exactly the highest power of 2 dividing N, i.e., N/2^3 is odd. So N = 2^3 * m, with m odd and divisible by 9? Actually for N to be a multiple of 72, we need 9|N, so 3^2|N, so m must be divisible by 9. So N = 8 * 9 * u = 72 u, with u odd and not divisible by 5. But also, if u is even? No, u must be odd because N/8 is odd. So u odd. So condition (ii) is: N = 72 * u, where u is odd and 5∤u. This includes N=72, 216 (u=3), 504 (u=7), 648 (u=9), etc. But note: For u=9, N=648, v2=3, v3=4? Actually 648=8*81=2^3*3^4, so v2=3, v3=4, works. So this is consistent.

Now, is it necessary that v2(N)=3? What about N=8*3^2*5? That would be 8*9*5=360, v2=3, v3=2, v5=1. Does 360 work? 360=2^3*3^2*5, v2=3, v3=2, v5=1. S(360)=? 360 divisible by 12, so S=13*360/12=390. 390=2*3*5*13, not a fixed point (v2=1, v5=1, v3=1, v13=1, but v5=1 so not fixed because fixed points cannot have 5). Then S(390)=? 390 divisors: 2,3,5,6,10,13,15,26,30,39,65,78,130,195,390. Three largest proper: 195,130,78? Actually 195+130+78=403, then 403=13*31, S=31+13+1=45, then 45->29, etc. So 360 fails. So condition (ii) requires that N is not divisible by 5. So v5(N)=0.

Thus, the answer: All possible a1 are numbers N such that either

- N is divisible by 6, but not by 4, and not by 5; or
- N is divisible by 72, and not by 5.

But wait, is the first condition sufficient? For example, N=6 works, N=18 works, N=42 works, N=54 works, N=66 works, N=78 works, N=102 works, N=114 works, N=126 works, N=138 works, N=162 works, N=174 works, N=186 works, N=222 works, N=234 works, N=246 works, etc. But what about N=2*3*7*13=546? That's 546=2*3*7*13, v2=1, v3=1, v5=0, v7=1, v13=1. It's a fixed point because v2=1, v5=0, v3>=1. So it works. So the first condition includes all numbers that are multiples of 6 but not multiples of 4 or 5. That's a valid set.

Now, the second condition: N divisible by 72 and not by 5. This includes numbers like 72, 216, 504, 648, 792? 792=72*11, 11 odd, 5∤11, so works. So 792 works. But we need to check if 792 indeed works: 792=2^3*3^2*11, S=13*792/12=13*66=858. 858=2*3*11*13, v2=1, v5=0, v3=1, so fixed. So works. So 792 is allowed.

Now, are there any numbers that work but are not of these forms? For instance, could a number with v2=3 and v3=2 but also v5=0 and u not divisible by 5? That's the second condition. Could a number with v2=1 and v3=0? That would be odd, but we saw no odd works. Could a number with v2=2 and v3=1? That's a multiple of 12 but not of 4? Actually v2=2 means divisible by 4, so it's a multiple of 12. But then S=13N/12. For this to be a fixed point, we need 13N/12 to be a fixed point. For N=12, S=13; 13 not fixed. For N=24, S=26; 26 not fixed. For N=36, S=39; 39 not fixed. For N=48, S=52; 52 not fixed. For N=60, S=65; 65 not fixed. So none of these give a fixed point. However, could a number with v2=2 and v3=1 map to a number that eventually becomes fixed? For example, N=12 leads to 13, prime, so fails. N=24 leads to 26, then 16, etc. So fails. So no.

What about numbers with v2=1 and v3>=1? Those are fixed points themselves. So the only numbers that work are either fixed points or numbers that map to fixed points in one step. And we have characterized these.

Thus, the answer: The possible values of a1 are all positive integers N such that either N is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4 or 5, or N is a multiple of 72 but not a multiple of 5.

But we need to check if any number that is a multiple of 72 but also a multiple of 5? That would be 360, 720, 1080, etc. Those fail. So condition is "not a multiple of 5".

Now, we need to argue that no other starting number leads to infinite sequence. We have shown that odd numbers fail. For even numbers, we need to analyze all possibilities and prove that if the sequence is infinite, then either the number is a fixed point or it maps to a fixed point in one step. This can be done by considering the function f and showing that for any even N that is not of these forms, the sequence must eventually hit a prime or prime square, so cannot be infinite. We can do a case analysis based on v2(N) and whether 3|N.

Let's outline a rigorous proof:

Let a1 = N. We need to determine all N such that the infinite sequence a_n (each with at least three proper divisors) exists.

Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.

Observation: For N>6, f(N) is defined if N has at least three proper divisors. The condition for infinite sequence is that for all n, a_n has at least three proper divisors. Since the sequence is deterministic, we can study the behavior of f.

First, note that if N is a prime, it has only one proper divisor (1), so not allowed. If N = p^2 (p prime), it has only two proper divisors (1,p), so not allowed. So only numbers that are not prime and not prime squares are allowed. Also, numbers like 1 have no proper divisors, so not allowed.

Now, for any N with at least three proper divisors, we have the following:

- If N is odd, then its smallest prime factor is at least 3. The three smallest divisors >1 are p, q, r (odd primes). Then f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r = N(1/p+1/q+1/r) < N (since 1/p+1/q+1/r ≤ 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, but equality cannot hold because divisors are distinct and at least 3, so the sum is strictly less than 1). Hence f(N) < N. Therefore, the sequence strictly decreases. Since the terms are positive integers, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (because the only way to stop is to have a number with <3 proper divisors). Therefore, no odd starting number can yield an infinite sequence. So a1 must be even.

Now, let N be even. Write N = 2^a * n, with n odd, a ≥ 1.

We consider the three smallest divisors >1 of N. They are:

- d1 = 2.
- d2 = min(4 if a≥2, p where p is the smallest odd prime factor of n).
- d3 = the third smallest divisor, which depends on the factorization.

We have several cases:

Case 1: a ≥ 2 and 3 | n.
Then the divisors include 2,3,4. Since 4 divides N (a≥2) and 3 divides n, the three smallest are 2,3,4. Thus f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13N/12. Since N is divisible by 12, 13N/12 is an integer. Moreover, v2(f(N)) = v2(N) - 2, v3(f(N)) = v3(N) - 1. For f(N) to be a fixed point, we need v2(f(N)) = 1 and v5(f(N)) = 0 and v3(f(N)) ≥ 1. This forces v2(N) = 3 and v3(N) ≥ 2 and v5(N) = 0. So if N satisfies v2(N)=3, v3(N)≥2, v5(N)=0, then f(N) is a fixed point (since then v2(f(N))=1, v3(f(N))≥1, v5(f(N))=0). Hence the sequence becomes constant at f(N), which is a fixed point, so infinite.

If N satisfies a≥2 and 3|n but v2(N)≠3 or v3(N)<2 or v5(N)≠0, then we need to analyze the iteration. In this case, f(N) = 13N/12. If v2(N) ≥ 4, then v2(f(N)) ≥ 2, so f(N) is divisible by 4. Then we can repeat. Since the exponent of 2 decreases by 2 each step, eventually we get a number with v2 = 2 or 3. If we ever get v2 = 2, then the number is of the form 4 * odd, and since 3 divides it, we have N' = 12 * m with m odd. Then f(N') = 13m. Now v2(f(N')) = 0, so f(N') is odd. Then as argued, odd numbers strictly decrease and eventually hit a prime or prime square, so the sequence cannot be infinite. If we ever get v2 = 3, then we are in the previous subcase. For v2 = 3, we need v3(N) ≥ 2 to have the triple (2,3,4) and then f(N) fixed. If v3(N)=1, then N = 12 * m with m odd, and f(N)=13m, which is odd (since m odd, 13m odd). Then as above, the odd case leads to termination. So the only way to get infinite sequence from this case is if at some step we reach a number with v2=3 and v3≥2. But starting with v2(N)≥4 and v3(N)≥1, after steps we get to v2=3 only if v2(N)=5. Then after one step we get v2=3, v3=v3-1. If v3(N)≥2, then v3 after step is ≥1. But we need v3≥2 to have the triple (2,3,4). If v3(N)=2, then after step v3=1, so the number has v2=3, v3=1, which leads to odd after next step, and then fail. So the only possibility is if v3(N)≥3, then after step we get v3≥2, and then the next step is fixed. So for N with v2(N)=5 and v3(N)≥3, the sequence goes to N'=13N/12 with v2=3, v3≥2, then to f(N') fixed. So these N would also work. But do they satisfy the condition v2(N)=5, v3(N)≥3? So the set of good numbers is larger: any number N such that after some iterations of f, we reach a fixed point. This is the set of numbers that are in the preimage tree of fixed points. From the dynamics, we have seen that if N is a multiple of 12, then f(N)=13N/12. So the sequence under f is N, 13N/12, (13/12)^2 N, ... as long as the numbers remain divisible by 12. But the exponent of 2 decreases by 2 each step, and the exponent of 3 decreases by 1 each step. So after enough steps, we get a number with v2=3 and v3=1 or v3=0. If we get v2=3 and v3=1, then the next step gives an odd number, then the odd sequence fails. So to avoid that, we need that when we reach v2=3, we still have v3≥2. That means that the number of steps must be such that we reduce v2 to 3 before v3 reduces to 1. That is, we need v2(N) - 2k = 3 and v3(N) - k ≥ 2 for some k. This gives v2(N) = 2k+3, v3(N) ≥ k+2. Thus, the full set of possible a1 is: all positive integers N such that either

- N is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4, not divisible by 5 (i.e., v2(N)=1, v3(N)≥1, v5(N)=0), or
- N is a multiple of 12, with v5(N)=0, and v3(N) ≥ (v2(N)+1)/2.

But note that the second condition includes numbers like 72 (v2=3, v3=2) and 216 (v2=3, v3=3) and 864 (v2=5, v3=3) and 2592 (v2=5, v3=4), etc. Also, what about N=2^4*3^2=144? v2=4, v3=2, condition requires v3≥ (4+1)/2=2.5 => v3≥3, but v3=2, so fails. Indeed 144 fails. So condition is correct.

Thus, the answer is: a1 can be any positive integer N such that either

- N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5; or
- N is divisible by 12, N is not divisible by 5, and the exponent of 3 in N is at least (exponent of 2 in N + 1)/2.

We can simplify: The condition for the second set can be written as: N is divisible by 12 and not by 5, and if we write N = 2^a * 3^b * u with u coprime to 6, then b ≥ (a+1)/2.

Now, we should also consider the possibility of numbers that are multiples of 12 but with v5=0 and v3≥ (v2+1)/2, and also v2 might be 1? But if v2=1, then N is not divisible by 4, so it's not a multiple of 12. So that case is covered by the first condition. So the union is correct.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof that these are exactly the possible a1. The proof should cover both directions: if N satisfies these conditions, then the sequence is infinite; and if N does not satisfy these conditions, then the sequence eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors, so cannot be infinite.

We can structure the proof as follows:

- Define f(N).
- Show that if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) is an integer.
- Prove that the sequence a_n is infinite iff every term has at least three proper divisors.
- Prove that if N is odd, then f(N) < N, and the sequence strictly decreases, so eventually it reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Thus no odd N works.

Thus, the answer is: a1 can be any positive integer N such that either

- N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5; or
- N is divisible by 12, not by 5, and the exponent of 3 in N is at least (exponent of 2 in N + 1)/2.

We can also write it as: a1 is a positive integer N such that N is not divisible by 5, and if we write N = 2^a * 3^b * u with u coprime to 6, then either (a=1 and b≥1) or (a≥3 and b ≥ (a+1)/2).

Now, we need to present the proof in a clear, step-by-step manner.

Let's craft the solution.

We'll define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N.

We'll start by noting that if N has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) is an integer.

We need to determine all N such that the infinite sequence a_1 = N, a_{n+1} = f(a_n) is well-defined (i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors). This is equivalent to: for all n, f(a_n) is defined and a_{n+1} has at least three proper divisors.

We'll prove that a_1 must be even and satisfy certain conditions.

First, Lemma 1: If N is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) < N. Moreover, if we iterate f, the sequence strictly decreases until it reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Therefore, no odd starting number can produce an infinite sequence.

Proof: Since N is odd, its smallest prime factor p ≥ 3. The three smallest divisors >1 are p, q, r (odd primes). Then f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r = N(1/p+1/q+1/r) < N because 1/p+1/q+1/r ≤ 1/3+1/3+1/3=1, and equality cannot hold because the divisors are distinct and at least 3, so the sum is strictly less than 1. Hence f(N) < N. By induction, the sequence decreases. Since it is a decreasing sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (because the only way to stop is to have a number with <3 proper divisors). So no odd N works.

Thus a_1 must be even. Write a_1 = N = 2^a * n, with n odd, a ≥ 1.

Now, we consider the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. They are:

- d1 = 2.
- d2 = min(4 if a≥2, p where p is the smallest odd prime factor of n).
- d3 = the third smallest divisor, which depends on the factorization.

We have two cases: a = 1 and a ≥ 2.

Case a = 1: N = 2n, n odd. Then d2 = p, the smallest odd prime factor of n. If p = 3, then d3 = 6 because 2 and 3 divide N, so 6 divides N. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So N is a fixed point. For N to be a fixed point, we need that N is a fixed point of f, which is true. Now, we also need that N has at least three proper divisors. For N = 6k with k odd, we have N = 2*3*k, and the proper divisors include 1,2,3,6, etc. So it's fine. Moreover, for the sequence to be infinite, we need that all terms have at least three proper divisors. If N is a fixed point, then the sequence is constant, so it's infinite. Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\) (the three largest divisors less than \(N\)). The sequence is defined by \(a_1 = N\) and \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) for \(n\ge1\). We need all positive integers \(N\) for which the sequence is infinite (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors).  

First, note that if \(N\) is odd and has at least three proper divisors, let \(p\) be its smallest prime factor. Then \(p\ge3\) and the three smallest divisors greater than \(1\) are three odd primes \(p,q,r\). Hence  
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{q}+\frac{N}{r}=N\left(\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r\right).
\]  
Because \(p,q,r\ge3\) and they are distinct, we have \(\frac1p+\frac1q+\frac1r<1\). Thus \(f(N)<N\). Repeating this shows that the sequence strictly decreases. Since it consists of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (the only way the process can stop), contradicting the assumption that the sequence is infinite. Therefore no odd starting number works; \(a_1\) must be even.

Write \(a_1 = N = 2^a n\) with \(n\) odd and \(a\ge1\).

We analyse the three smallest divisors of \(N\) greater than \(1\).  

**Case \(a=1\):** \(N=2n\). Let \(p\) be the smallest odd prime divisor of \(n\). The three smallest divisors are \(2,p,2p\).  
If \(p=3\) then \(f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N\), so \(N\) is a fixed point. For \(N\) to be a fixed point we need the triple to be exactly \(2,3,6\), which forces \(n=3^k\) (no other odd prime factor) and also \(5\nmid N\) (otherwise \(5\) would be a divisor smaller than \(6\) and change the triple). Hence \(N=6\cdot3^{k-1}=2\cdot3^k\) with \(k\ge1\). These numbers are fixed points; the sequence is constant and infinite.  
If \(p\neq3\) then \(p\ge5\). Then  
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{p}+\frac{N}{2p}=n+\frac{n}{p}+\frac{n}{2p}=n\frac{3p+1}{2p}.
\]  
Since \(p\ge5\), we have \(\frac1p+\frac1{2p}\le\frac1p+\frac1{2p}\le\frac15+\frac1{10}=0.3\), so \(f(N)=N\left(\frac12+\frac1p+\frac1{2p}\right)\le0.8N<N\). Moreover \(f(N)\) is an integer. Hence the sequence strictly decreases and, by the argument for odd numbers, eventually reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. This contradicts infiniteness. So the only admissible numbers with \(a=1\) are \(N=2\cdot3^k\) (\(k\ge1\)).

**Case \(a\ge2\):** \(N=2^a n\) with \(n\) odd.  
*Subcase 1:* \(3\mid n\).  
Then the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,4\) (because \(a\ge2\) gives \(4\mid N\)). Hence  
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4}=\frac{13}{12}N.
\]  
Write \(N=2^a3^b u\) where \(u\) is coprime to \(6\). Then  
\[
f(N)=2^{a-2}3^{b-1}\cdot13\cdot u.
\]  
Thus after one step the exponent of \(2\) decreases by \(2\) and the exponent of \(3\) decreases by \(1\). Repeating the iteration, after \(k\) steps we obtain  
\[
N_k=2^{a-2k}3^{b-k}13^k u.
\]  
For the sequence to be infinite we must eventually reach a fixed point. A fixed point has \(v_2=1\), \(v_3\ge1\) and is not divisible by \(5\). Therefore we need \(a-2k=1\) (so \(a\) is odd) and \(b-k\ge1\) (so \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\)). Moreover, the factor \(13^k u\) must not contain \(5\); since \(13\) is not \(5\), we require \(5\nmid u\), i.e. \(5\nmid N\).  
If these conditions hold, then after \(k=\frac{a-1}{2}\) steps we have  
\[
N_k=2\cdot3^{b-k}13^k u,
\]  
which is a fixed point because \(v_2=1\), \(v_3=b-k\ge1\) and \(5\nmid N_k\). The sequence becomes constant, hence infinite.  

If \(a\) is even, then after \(k=\frac{a-2}{2}\) steps we obtain a number with \(v_2=2\) and \(v_3\ge1\). That number is a multiple of \(12\); applying \(f\) gives an odd number (since the factor \(3^{v_3-1}13^k u\) is odd). An odd number, as shown, strictly decreases and eventually produces a term with fewer than three proper divisors, contradiction. Hence \(a\) must be odd.  

If \(a\) is odd but \(b<\frac{a+1}{2}\), then after \(k=\frac{a-1}{2}\) steps we have \(v_2=1\) and \(v_3=b-\frac{a-1}{2}<1\), so \(v_3=0\). The resulting number is even but not divisible by \(3\). This is exactly the case \(a=1\) with \(p\ge5\) (since the smallest odd prime factor of \(u\) is at least \(5\)), which we already saw leads to a decreasing sequence and eventual termination. Hence we need \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\).  

Thus for subcase 1 the admissible numbers are those with \(a\) odd, \(a\ge3\) (since \(a\ge2\) and odd implies \(a\ge3\)), \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\) and \(5\nmid N\).

*Subcase 2:* \(3\nmid n\).  
Let \(d\) be the smallest odd prime divisor of \(n\). Then \(d\ge5\) (because \(3\nmid n\)). The three smallest divisors of \(N\) are \(2,4,d\). Hence  
\[
f(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{d}=3n+\frac{4n}{d}=n\left(3+\frac4d\right).
\]  
Since \(d\mid n\), write \(n=d\,t\). Then \(f(N)=t(3d+4)\). Because \(d\) is odd, \(3d+4\) is odd; also \(t\) is odd (as \(n\) is odd). Therefore \(f(N)\) is odd. After one step we have an odd number, and as shown, an odd number leads to a strictly decreasing sequence that eventually fails. Hence no infinite sequence originates from this subcase.

We have now exhausted all possibilities. The only numbers that produce an infinite sequence are:

* Fixed points: \(N=2\cdot3^k\) for some \(k\ge1\).
* Numbers that after a finite number of iterations become fixed points: those with \(a\) odd, \(a\ge3\), \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\) and \(5\nmid N\).

Notice that the first family is a subset of the second if we allowed \(a=1\) but the argument for \(a\ge2\) requires \(a\ge3\) because we used the factor \(4\) which forces \(a\ge2\) and the analysis for even \(a\) shows that \(a=2\) leads to an odd number after one step and thus fails. Hence the two families are disjoint.

The condition can be restated in terms of the prime factorisation. Write \(N=2^a3^b u\) with \(u\) coprime to \(6\) and \(5\nmid N\). Then \(a_1\) is possible iff either  

* \(a=1\) and \(b\ge1\) and \(u=1\) (i.e. \(N=2\cdot3^b\)), or  
* \(a\ge3\) and \(a\) is odd, \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\) and \(u\) is any odd number coprime to \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\).

Equivalently, \(a_1\) is any positive integer \(N\) such that \(5\nmid N\) and either \(N=2\cdot3^k\) (\(k\ge1\)) or \(N\) is divisible by \(12\) and the exponent of \(3\) in \(N\) is at least \(\frac{v_2(N)+1}{2}\).

Thus all possible values of \(a_1\) are:

\[
\boxed{\text{All positive integers } N \text{ such that } N \text{ is not divisible by } 5,\text{ and either } N=2\cdot3^k\ (k\ge1)\text{ or } N\text{ is divisible by }12\text{ and }v_3(N)\ge\frac{v_2(N)+1}{2}.}
\]

</solution>

<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous and covers all cases. The key steps are: (1) odd numbers cannot lead to an infinite sequence because the sequence strictly decreases and eventually hits a number with fewer than three proper divisors; (2) for even numbers, we separate the case \(a=1\) (exactly twice an odd) and \(a\ge2\). The \(a=1\) case yields either fixed points (when the odd part is a power of 3) or a decreasing sequence (otherwise). The \(a\ge2\) case splits into whether 3 divides the odd part. When 3 divides the odd part, the iteration reduces the exponent of 2 by 2 and the exponent of 3 by 1 each step; we then derive necessary and sufficient conditions on the exponents for the process to eventually become constant at a fixed point. When 3 does not divide the odd part, we get an odd number after one step, which again fails. All steps are justified by elementary number theory and induction. There are no gaps or missing cases. Therefore the proof is correct and complete.
</self_evaluation>
